Title: Self-Distillation is Optimal Among Spectral Shrinkage Estimators in Spiked Covariance Models

URL Source: https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.17778v1

Published Time: Tue, 19 May 2026 01:49:03 GMT

Number of Pages: 103

Markdown Content:
# Self-Distillation is Optimal Among Spectral Shrinkage Estimators in Spiked Covariance Models 

Radu Lecoiu 1, Debarghya Mukherjee 2, ∗, Pragya Sur 1, ∗,† 1Department of Statistics, Harvard University 

> 2

Department of Mathematics & Statistics, Boston University 

May 19, 2026 

Abstract 

Self-distillation has emerged as a promising technique for improving model performance in modern machine learning systems. We develop the statistical foundations of self-distillation in spiked covariance models, by introducing and analyzing a broad class of estimators, namely spectral shrinkage estimators. We establish that for spiked covariance matrices with s spikes, 

s-step self-distillation achieves optimal performance among spectral shrinkage estimators, out-performing well-known estimators in statistics and machine learning. Moreover, we show that 

s steps are necessary for optimality: any (s − k)-step distilled estimator is strictly suboptimal for 1 ≤ k ≤ s. For the special subclass of isotropic covariances, we show that optimally tuned Ridge regression performs best among spectral shrinkage estimators. We also study a feder-ated approach where multiple data centers share spectral shrinkage estimators and a common server seeks to aggregate them to achieve optimal performance. In this case, we find that the best local rule again takes the form of self-distillation, though it differs from the optimal rule when data are hosted centrally on a single server. Together, our results elucidate why self-distillation improves predictive performance and provide a broader statistical framework connecting it with classical shrinkage-based methods.

# 1 Introduction 

Distillation is one of the dominant training paradigms in modern AI systems. It allows to trans-fer knowledge from large, complex models to smaller ones that can be deployed to many users. Originally proposed in [1–3], the concept has been successfully applied across a wide variety of settings, ranging from theorem proving to real-time disaster damage assessment [4, 5]. Despite these practical successes, the statistical foundations underlying when and why distillation works remain seriously underdeveloped. In this paper, we bridge this substantial gap by developing a rigorous theory for self-distillation in the setting of high-dimensional regression. We consider a supervised learning problem where the responses are generated from a linear model conditional on the covariates. We are interested in covariates that are high-dimensional. Self-distillation refers to the subclass of distillation strategies where the model we distill from shares the same structure as the model we distill to. To illustrate in simple statistical terms: given training data, one might compute a preferred shrinkage estimator—say, ridge regression. Self-distillation uses this initial estimator to train new ridge estimators, encouraging each to align with those obtained in the previous stage. While this may seem a counter-intuitive strategy that re-uses 3data multiple times, self-distillation has been empirically shown to perform remarkably well [6– 8]. In this paper, we develop a rigorous statistical theory that explains why self-distillation works and when it outperforms the first-stage estimator. In particular, we establish that self-distillation achieves optimal prediction error among a large class of shrinkage-based estimators. To the best of our knowledge, this paper provides the first such optimality result for self-distillation under high-dimensional statistical models. We further expand our analysis to a decentralized learning setup, where data is distributed across multiple clients and communication with a central server is restricted due to privacy and budgetary constraints. Such settings are commonly studied under the broad paradigms of dis-tributed learning [9–12] and federated learning [13–15], where the goal is to leverage information across clients without requiring direct sharing of raw data. Federated learning combined with distillation—self-distillation in particular—has gained con-siderable popularity in recent years, owing to its strong empirical performance across a wide range of tasks (e.g., see [16–18] and the recent survey [19]). Despite this growing body of literature and its practical success, a rigorous statistical theory explaining when and why such distillation-based federated procedures are effective remains underdeveloped. In this paper, we also develop rigorous statistical underpinnings for a federated procedure where each client computes a local spectral shrinkage estimator and a central server linearly aggregates the transmitted estimators. Our main contributions are summarized as follows. • We introduce a class of spectral shrinkage-based estimators (Definition 2.4) and establish that self-distillation achieves optimal out-of-sample risk in this class when the covariate dis-tribution is not isotropic. Specifically, working within spiked covariance models [20], we show that for covariate distributions with s spikes, s-step self-distillation is optimal. (The-orem 3.4 (a)). Interestingly, this superiority no longer holds in the isotropic setting, where ridge regression is itself optimal (see Lemma 3.2 and subsequent discussion). • We further establish that for s spikes in the underlying covariate distribution, s − k-step self-distillation is sub-optimal for any 0 < k ≤ s (Theorem 3.4 (b)). Thus, intuitively, each step in the self-distillation process allows recovery of one spike direction. • The shrinkage-based estimators introduced in Definition 2.4 allow us to compare the per-formance of self-distillation against other well-known procedures such as ridge regression, minimum norm interpolation, and principal components regression. We establish that self-distillation strictly outperforms these alternatives under spiked covariance models (Theo-rem 3.5). In fact, with an appropriately chosen number of steps, self-distillation is almost uniquely optimal (we clarify the notion of uniqueness in Theorem 3.1). • In Section 3.1, we extend our analysis to a decentralized setting where each client computes a local spectral shrinkage estimator and transmits it to a central server that linearly aggregates the received estimators. We characterize the limiting prediction risk of this aggregated esti-mator and derive the corresponding optimal local shrinkage rules and aggregation weights. We find that the optimal local rule is again realized by self-distillation; however, the locally optimal shrinkage rule in the federated setting differs from that for a single client (the focus of the first part of the paper), reflecting the new bias–variance tradeoff induced by cross-client aggregation (Theorem 3.9). 

> Here, 0-step self-distillation refers to the initial shrinkage estimator

4A key ingredient in our analysis—and one that may be of independent interest— is a new random-matrix result concerning quadratic forms involving products of functions of distinct sample covariance matrices (Theorem 3.10). Throughout, we operate within the proportional asymptotics regime, where the number of features and samples grows at a comparable rate. This regime has become increasingly popular in high-dimensional statistics and machine learning theory, owing to its ability to capture new phenomena observed in high dimensions, explain empirical behavior seen in modern machine learning systems, and produce new inference and prediction methods with impressive practical performance [21–40]. The framework has also yielded powerful tools for studying the behavior of high-dimensional random matrices, which would be indispensable for our work. Before proceed-ing, we situate our work within the existing literature on self-distillation. We study a setting where the covariates and responses are sampled i.i.d. from a population. In the form of self-distillation we consider, at each stage, an estimator is learned by training on the original data while borrowing information from the estimator obtained in the previous stage. This is encapsulated by the weighted loss function (2.3): at stage t, ξt denotes the weight on the component that learns from the previous stage estimator, while (1 − ξt) denotes the weight on the component that learns directly from the training data. Prior work most closely related to ours include [41, 42]. The former studies the optimal choice for ξt while constraining estimators at each stage to use the same tuning parameter as the pre-vious stage. This significantly limits the class of estimators self-distillation can capture—indeed, we demonstrate later that the optimal self-distillation strategy uses different tuning parameters for different stages (Theorem 3.4(a)). On the other hand, [42] studies a two-step empirical risk minimization procedure that generalizes self-distillation and derives a precise high-dimensional characterization of the second-stage test error for linear models under Gaussian-mixture features. But, in its present form, their analysis does not extend to the multi-step self-distillation setting we consider here. Several other lines of work study problems adjacent to, but not directly related to, our setting. For instance, [43–48] study pure distillation (where ξt = 1). A parallel literature considers the case of fixed design and low dimensions [49–51]. Among these, [46, 49] restrict their analyses to a single distillation step, unlike ours. A separate line of work considers regenerating synthetic labels at each stage from the model fitted in the previous stage [52–54], studying either the impact of synthetic data on the long-run generalization error or the optimal rule for aggregating synthetic and original labels. On a different note, [55] studies connections between knowledge distillation and early stop-ping, while [56] provides a replica analysis of knowledge distillation in Gaussian mixture mod-els. Finally, [57] formulates distillation as a PAC-style imitation problem between two hypothesis classes; this work is closer in spirit to model compression [1] than to distillation in the sense of [2]. A related body of literature investigates semi-supervised learning and self-training [58–66]. As the papers surveyed in this and the preceding paragraph address problems that differ substantially from those studied here, we refrain from discussing these references further. The rest of the paper is organized as follows: Section 2 introduces our problem setup and other preliminaries, including formal definitions of self-distillation and the spectral shrinkage class. Sec-tion 3 presents our main results with supporting experiments in Section 4. Section 5 presents the main novelties in our proofs. Section 6 concludes the paper with a discussion of future directions. All proofs are presented in Appendices A–J. 52 Preliminaries

Spiked covariance models have been extensively studied in high-dimensional statistics since the seminal work of [20] and have become a standard framework for analyzing principal com-ponent methods and related spectral procedures. Although the model represents a special case of a general anisotropic covariance matrix, it is already rich enough to capture the key phenom-ena governing self-distillation in anisotropic settings. In particular, as we show below, even a finite-spike perturbation away from isotropy yields qualitatively different conclusions regarding the superiority of self-distillation over classical procedures (e.g., ridge regression and principal components regression (PCR)) compared to the isotropic setting. Under Assumption 2.1, the eigenvalues of bΣ exhibit a well-understood limiting structure. In the isotropic case Σ = σ20 Ip, the Marchenko–Pastur law [67] states that the empirical spectral distribution of bΣ converges almost surely to a limiting measure supported on the compact interval 

Sc = [ σ20 (1 − √c)2, σ20 (1 + √c)2], known as the bulk, and additionally has a point mass at 0 when 

c > 1. In the spiked model, [68] shows that sufficiently strong spikes generate additional outlier eigenvalues that escape the bulk, converging to deterministic locations outside Sc. We collect this structure in the following definition. 

Definition 2.2 (Limiting spectral support) . Denote the bulk support of the Marchenko–Pastur distribu-tion by 

Sc = σ20 (1 − √c)2, σ20 (1 + √c)2.6Now, for each spike δj satisfying δj > σ20

Assumption 2.3. Suppose that Σ is of the above spiked form and that the following conditions hold: 1. (Distinct spikes.) The spike strengths δ1, . . . , δr are positive and distinct. Moreover,

∥β0∥2 ∈ (0, ∞).Assumption 2.3 (1) rules out an edge case where the product of two spike strengths exactly equals cσ40 . This is a mild condition that, importantly, does not require the spike strengths to lie either above or below the BBP threshold. Our theory allows all, some, or none of the spikes to exceed the BBP threshold. The assumption is imposed only to avoid boundary cases and to keep the presentation technically transparent. Our qualitative conclusion on the superiority of self-distillation remains valid even when this condition is violated, although the resulting risk characterization is less clean; see Section 6 for further discussion. Assumption 2.3 (2) is a mild compatibility condition between Σ and β0. Specifically, it ensures that β0 has a non-zero projection onto each spike direction, so that each spike carries information relevant for learning β0, and that 

β0 does not lie entirely within the span of the spike directions.

2.3 Introducing the Spectral Shrinkage Class

This representation shows that multi-step self-distillation belongs to a broader family of estimators obtained by applying a shrinkage function f to the spectrum of bΣ. Our primary goal is to develop a rigorous theory for self-distillation in high-dimensional regression and to characterize its performance relative to classical statistical estimators (e.g., ridge, PCR, etc.). F . In Lemma 3.3, we show that the self-distilled estimator belongs to BF . We briefly discuss other commonly used estimators that fall within this class. 1. Ridge-regularized estimator. For any λ, the ridge estimator

Principal components regression. Principal components regression retains the eigenvectors of bΣ corresponding to eigenvalues that exceed a threshold τ > 0, and then performs least squares on the resulting subspace. This yields 

bβPCR (τ) = f (bΣ) X⊤y

n , f (x) = x−11(x ≥ τ). (2.12) Having defined the general spectral shrinkage class (Definition 2.4), we turn to specifying func-tions f that yield well-behaved estimators. Since f takes the eigenvalues of bΣ as its argument, a natural requirement is that f behaves well wherever the spectrum of bΣ concentrates, namely on S+ 

> c

. To ensure this, we impose the following assumption on F . Throughout the paper, for each η > 0 we define the η-neighborhood of a point x ≥ 0 as (max (0, x − η), x + η), and the 

η-neighborhood of a bounded interval [a, b] ⊂ [0, ∞) as (max (0, a − η), b + η).9Assumption 2.5. Recall Sc and Ac from Definition 2.2. The class F consists of all functions f : [0, ∞) →

R ∪ { ∞} satisfying the following conditions: 1. there exists η > 0, independent of n, such that f is continuous on an η-neighborhood of Sc.2. there exists η > 0, independent of n, such that f is continuous on an η-neighborhood of every point in Ac.

Assumption 2.5 guarantees that f is continuous on a fixed η-neighborhood of every point in 

S+ 

> c

. By [68], almost surely for all sufficiently large n, every eigenvalue of bΣ falls within such a neighborhood. In particular, f is finite and continuous at every eigenvalue of bΣ, so the decompo-sition of bβ f in (2.9) contains all eigenvector directions of bΣ.Furthermore, Assumption 2.5 covers the common estimator examples we provided for spectral shrinkage estimators, whether directly in finite samples or asymptotically. For instance, Ridge regression, and more generally, the self-distillation shrinkage function ft corresponding to bβ(t) 

> SD

in (2.5), belong to F as long as −λ0, −λ1, . . . , −λt ∈ R \ S + 

> c

. Since the analyst chooses these tuning parameters, we can easily ensure this condition is true. Additionally, in Theorem 3.4, we show that the optimal self-distillation strategy for spiked covariance matrices involves ridge parameters 

λ0, . . . , λt that satisfy this condition. The gradient descent shrinkage function fT from (2.10) is a polynomial, hence continuous ev-erywhere, and therefore belongs to F . For the min-norm interpolator (2.11), the shrinkage func-tion has a pole at 0. Since 0 ∈ S + 

> c

whenever c > 1, this estimator does not belong to F in this regime. Similarly, for PCR (2.12), the threshold τ is in general random, depending on the eigen-values of the sample covariance matrix, and hence cannot belong to the deterministic class F .However, asymptotically, both estimators behave like estimators in BF corresponding to smooth approximations of their respective shrinkage functions. We make this precise in Lemma F.1 for the min-norm interpolator, and in Lemma G.2 and Lemma G.4 for PCR. 

2.4 Out-of-Sample Prediction Risk 

The main message of our paper is that suitably tuned self-distillation achieves uniquely opti-mal prediction performance within the spectral shrinkage class, thereby outperforming the well-known estimators described above. To quantify this, we study the prediction risk of spectral shrinkage estimators, defined as

2.5 Extension to the Decentralized Setup 

Thus far, we have presented our framework in a centralized setting, where all samples are stored on a single machine, and the estimator is constructed from the full dataset. As discussed in Sec-tion 1, we extend our analysis to a decentralized setting with K > 1 clients and a central server: each client constructs a local spectral shrinkage estimator from its own data, transmits it to the central server, and the server then aggregates these local estimators. Since this extension requires additional notation to reflect the multi-client setup, we defer these details and the corresponding results to Section 3.1.

Prior work has studied the asymptotic risk of ridge regression and its optimal tuning under high-dimensional asymptotics [29, 76, 77], but these differ from our result in important respects. First, an optimality result of a similar spirit was proved in [76]: under Gaussian isotropic designs and with risk measured unconditionally over both X and ε, [76] proves that optimally tuned ridge regression is asymptotically minimax over the sphere {β0 : ∥β0∥2 = r}. This is the closest antecedent to our result, though the assumptions are substantially stronger. Subsequently, [77] places a prior distribution on β0 and, conditionally on X, characterizes the asymptotic risk of ridge regression and identifies the optimal λ > 0 within the ridge family. Finally, [29] works with a fixed β0, conditionally on X, and similarly characterizes the asymptotic risk of ridge regression and identifies the optimal λ > 0 within the ridge family. Neither [77] nor [29] address optimality over a broader class of estimators. Our result differs from the aforementioned papers in two key aspects. First, we work condi-tionally on X and y with a fixed β0. Second, Lemma 3.2 provides optimality of tuned ridge over the full spectral shrinkage class, and without requiring Gaussian assumptions as in [76]. The situation, however, changes fundamentally in the anisotropic case, when the covariance matrix of X has s ≥ 1 spikes. As we will establish, s-step self-distillation yields the optimal shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

in this regime. To this end, we first prove a structural lemma showing that multi-step self-distillation remains within the spectral shrinkage family BF . In particular, given any k ∈ N and parameters 

Θk := ( λ0, . . . , λk, ξ1, . . . , ξk),the k-step self-distilled estimator can be written in the form bβ f for a suitable shrinkage function f

depending on Θk, as follows:

The proof is deferred to Appendix C.2. Having established that a multi-step self-distillation procedure produces an estimator in BF , we next show that under a spiked covariance model, the optimal shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

Theorem 3.4 (Optimality of self-distillation) . Under Assumptions 2.1–2.3, suppose that Σ has s ≥ 1

non-zero spikes. Then: (a) there exists a choice of Ridge parameters (λ0, . . . , λs) and distillation weights (ξ1, . . . , ξs) such that the optimal shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

Θs = ( λ0, . . . , λs, ξ1, . . . , ξs)

> c

.

12 (b) s steps of self-distillation are, in general, necessary for optimality. Specifically, there exist spike strengths δ1, . . . , δs and σ20 , σ2 

> ε

such that the optimal shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

cannot be realized by any 

(s − k)-step self-distillation procedure for any 1 ≤ k ≤ s. 

Theorem 3.4 shows that self-distillation is both sufficient and, in general, necessary for achiev-ing the optimal spectral shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

. Moreover, the optimal rule f pred  

> ∗

can be implemented through s rounds of self-distillation where s is the number of spikes in the underlying covariance matrix. On the other hand, any procedure with fewer than s steps fails to attain the optimal risk. As an immediate consequence, classical ridge regression, corresponding to zero distillation steps, is provably suboptimal as soon as s ≥ 1. We defer the proof of part (a) to Appendix D. The proof of part (b) is constructive and is deferred to Appendix E. We highlight that the optimal self-distillation representation involves negative ridge parame-ters λt < 0. To the best of our knowledge, all existing theoretical analyses of self-distillation restrict attention to positive ridge parameters (c.f. [41, 50] and references cited therein). Notably, our re-sults hold without this restriction. In fact, Theorem 3.4(a) shows that among the ridge parameters appearing in any optimal self-distillation representation, only one is positive. This corroborates and extends earlier observations in the pure ridge literature, which show that the optimal ridge hyperparameter may be negative [70–75]. The next theorem shows that multi-step self-distillation attains a strictly smaller limiting pre-diction risk than a number of popular alternatives: 

Theorem 3.5. Suppose Assumption 2.3 holds and s ≥ 1. Then the estimator associated with f pred  

> ∗

has strictly smaller asymptotic out-of-sample prediction risk than each of the following procedures: (a) the min-norm interpolator (equivalently, ordinary least squares when c < 1); (b) any self-distillation procedure that uses a common ridge parameter across all iterations, including ridge regression as the case of zero distillation steps; (c) gradient descent with any finite number of steps; (d) principal components regression with any fixed number of retained components; (e) principal components regression with a sequence m n of retained components with m n/p → τ ∈0, min (1, 1/ c) as n → ∞.

The proofs of parts (a), (b), and (c) can be found in Appendix F, while the proofs of (d) and (e) are deferred to Appendix G. Theorem 3.5 emphasizes that self-distillation outperforms not only ridge regression but a broad range of well-known procedures. In particular, while classically one might expect PCR to perform well in the presence of a spiked covariance structure, since it ex-plicitly exploits the dominant spectral directions, Theorem 2.3 shows that the optimal self-distilled estimator strictly outperforms PCR. Hence, under the spiked covariance models, the optimal spec-tral transformation is neither a simple inverse as in ridge nor a thresholding rule as in PCR, but rather a more intricate rational function that can be obtained via self-distillation. Thus far, we characterized the optimal shrinkage rule that minimizes the limiting prediction risk. We now show that analogous results hold for the limiting estimation risk, defined as:

Theorem 3.9 shows that the federated problem retains the same fundamental feature as the centralized one: the optimal local rule is again a rational shrinkage function, and can be implemented by finitely many steps of self-distillation. In the special case K = 1, we recover the optimal shrinkage rule from Theorem 3.1. In the isotropic case 

Σ = Ip, we recover the optimal aggregation of ridge estimators studied in the distributed ridge setup [12]. Moreover, Theorem 3.9 reveals a genuinely new phenomenon created by decentralization in the anisotropic case with K > 1 clients: the optimal local shrinkage rule f ∗ 

> K

for each client differs from the single-client optimum f pred  

> ∗

obtained in Theorem 3.1. This distinction is noteworthy from a methodological perspective. In a centralized environment (single-client), the client chooses a shrinkage rule to optimize their own bias-variance tradeoff based on their sample covariance matrix. In the federated setting, by contrast, the local estimators are computed from independent sample covariance matrices and are then combined at the server. The interaction between local shrinkage and cross-client aggregation, therefore, creates a new optimization problem, and the final solution differs from that of the single-client problem. We further illustrate this phenomenon using Figure 1, which plots the values of {b(K) 

> j

}0≤j≤s

for varying K, with s = 2. Note that the optimal shrinkage function in both the single client (K = 1) and multi-client cases ( K > 1), (3.1) and (3.9), depend on these coefficients. Thus, they vary significantly in the number of clients, as demonstrated by the strict monotonicity of these coefficients as a function of K.16 The key technical ingredient underlying Theorem 3.9 is the following result, which extends our spectral shrinkage machinery from one sample covariance matrix to products involving two independent sample covariance matrices. For the following result, we extend Definition 2.4 as follows. Let Fcℓ and Fck denote the function class introduced in Assumption 2.5, corresponding to limiting ratios cℓ and ck respectively. 

Theorem 3.10. Let bΣℓ and bΣk denote the sample covariance matrices for the ℓth and k th client respectively. Suppose p /nℓ → cℓ and p /nk → ck where c ℓ, ck ∈ (0, ∞). Then, for any ϕ ∈ F cℓ and ψ ∈ F ck , we have:

where F MP, c and F δj, c denote the Marchenko-Pastur law and the spiked probability measures introduced in (A.4) with limiting aspect ratio c. 

The proof is deferred to Appendix J. Taken together, Theorems 3.9 and 3.10 show that the role of self-distillation extends beyond the single-client setting. Even in this simplified federated model, the optimal distributed strategy is obtained by combining local self-distilled estimators through an explicit aggregation rule. We view this as a first step toward a more general statis-tical theory of federated learning with distillation, allowing heterogeneous client distributions, iterative communication, or more general covariance profiles.

# 4 Simulation Studies 

4.1 Isotropic Design 

We first demonstrate our results for the case of isotropic design, where Lemma 3.2 shows that an optimally tuned ridge is optimal in our spectral shrinkage class. We generate synthetic data as follows. We sample X ∈ Rn×p whose rows are i.i.d. draws from N (0, Ip), and draw ε ∼ N (0, σ2 

> ε

In)

independently of X. Both X and ε are drawn once and held fixed throughout. The response is 17 then y = X β0 + ε, where β0 ∈ Rp is the true parameter vector, obtained by drawing a vector uniformly at random from the unit sphere in Rp and rescaling to have norm r = ∥β0∥2. We subsequently hold the signal fixed. We compare the performance of Ridge regression for λ > 0and one step of self-distillation. For self-distillation, for each fixed λ, we perform a grid search on 

ξ1, λ1 (see equation 2.3). We also analyze principal components regression: we compare four cases where the number of retained components is mn ∈ { 1, 50, 100, 200 } to highlight both the finite and proportional number of components as proved in Theorem 3.5(c) and (d). The prediction risk for each model is estimated using 200 i.i.d. test samples drawn from the same distribution. 

Figure 2: Sub-optimality of self-distillation and principal components regression in the isotropic setting. Left graph: n = 1500, p = 600, ∥β0∥ = 3, σε = 2.5, σ0 = 1. Right graph: n = 1000, p =

2000, ∥β0∥ = 2, σε = 1, σ0 = 1. As proved in [41], for each λ that is not a critical point of the asymptotic Ridge curve, self-distillation with λ1 = λ achieves strictly lower prediction risk than the original ridge estimator. We note that this is still the case without the restriction that λ1 = λ, as clearly illustrated in Fig-ure 2: the orange curve (self-distillation) and the blue curve (ridge) meet only at the local minimum of Ridge, with strict improvement everywhere else. Moreover, in both the underparametrized regime (left panel) and the overparametrized regime (right panel), no estimator achieves lower risk than the optimally tuned ridge. PCR falls well short of this benchmark, while self-distillation at λ̸ = λ∗ does not improve upon ridge at λ∗. Nevertheless, from a practical standpoint, self-distillation with suitable ξ1 is appealing precisely because it uniformly dominates the ridge risk curve across all values of the tuning parameter; In particular, this implies that self-distillation can be advantageous in finite samples even in the isotropic case, where the population-optimal tuning parameter λ∗ is unknown and must be estimated, thereby incurring additional estimation error. 

4.2 Anisotropic Design 

4.2.1 Closed-Form Limits 

We next conduct experiments in the one-spike model Σ = Ip + δvv ⊤ and the two-spike model 

Σ = Ip + δ1v1v⊤ 

> 1

+ δ2v2v⊤ 

> 2

. Lemma B.1 allows us to compute the limiting prediction risk for any 18 bβ f with f ∈ F . We demonstrate the results of Theorem 3.4 by numerically computing the limiting prediction risk of optimally tuned ridge regression and optimally tuned self-distillation bβ f for 

f = f pred  

> ∗

. The results are displayed in Figure 3. In panels (a) and (b), for small values of δ, the two curves nearly coincide, which is consistent with our theory: as δ → 0 the covariance becomes isotropic, and an optimally tuned ridge is known to be optimal in that regime. As δ increases, we notice the advantages of self-distillation. On the other hand, in panels (c) and (d), even as δ2 → 0, the two curves differ significantly. This is expected: as δ2 → 0, the model reduces to a one-spike model with the fixed spike δ1, for which an optimally tuned ridge is already known to be strictly suboptimal. Regarding the improvements, the maximum percentage gain of self-distillation over optimally tuned ridge is 44.55% in panel (a), 3.78% in panel (b), 29.53% in panel (c) and 12.31% in panel (d). The improvements are considerably more pronounced in the overparametrized regime, suggest-ing that the benefits of self-distillation are amplified when p ≫ n.

4.2.2 Optimal Self-Distillation Parameters 

Using the construction from Theorem 3.4, we identify the optimal parameters (λ∗ 

> 0

, λ∗ 

> 1

, ξ∗) in the one-spike model Σ = Ip + δvv ⊤ such that f pred  

> ∗

is the shrinkage function corresponding to a self-distillation estimator. We plot these parameters as functions of the spike strength δ over a grid of values in (0, 20 ).We note from Figure 4a that ξ∗ starts near zero when δ ≈ 0, which is expected: setting the weight parameter to zero reduces our estimator to a ridge estimator tuned at the second step, which is known to be optimal under isotropic designs. As δ increases, ξ∗ becomes negative and remains so before returning asymptotically to zero as δ grows further. Thus, the optimal weight for self-distillation in this case is actually negative. Turning to Figure 4b, we observe that λ∗ 

> 0

is always negative, and its absolute value grows at the same rate as the outlier x⋆ 

> 1

from Definition 2.2. We know that the largest eigenvalue of the sample covariance converges to x⋆ 

> 1

if and only if δ > √c, which is precisely the BBP phase transition threshold [68]. The value δ = √c is marked on the horizontal axis of Figure 4b for reference. Interestingly, the growth rates of −λ∗ 

> 0

and x⋆ 

> 1

coincide even for values of δ below this BBP phase transition threshold, though the outlier cannot be estimated from bΣ in that regime.

Finally, optimal self-distillation, with parameters from Theorem 3.1, uniformly dominates other estimators across all values of δ.While our theoretical results are established for spectral shrinkage estimators and do not cover the Lasso, it is natural to ask whether the general phenomenon that self-distillation strictly im-proves over the initial estimator continues to hold beyond this class. Figure 6 provides numerical evidence that self-distillation improves the Lasso in the one-spike case. Specifically, we observe that optimally tuned SD-Lasso consistently outperforms Lasso across all values of λ0, and moreover, the optimal teacher parameter λ0 for SD-Lasso differs from the optimal parameter for the plain Lasso, indicating that self-distillation benefits from a delib-erately mistuned teacher. These observations suggest that the optimality of self-distillation is a robust phenomenon that extends beyond the spectral shrinkage settings covered by our theory. 21 Figure 5: Prediction risk of Ridge, optimal self-distillation (SD), and PCR as a function of the spike strength δ ∈ (0.1, 10 ), estimated via simulation. Step II: (Optimality of self-distillation) Our next contribution is to show that f pred  

> ∗

can be at-tained by an s-step self-distillation procedure. Moreover, we show that there exists a choice of 

{(δj, vj)}1≤j≤s for which s steps of self-distillation are genuinely necessary to attain f pred  

> ∗

, in the sense that no procedure with fewer than s steps can recover bβ f pred 

> ∗

, regardless of the choice of tuning parameters. To this end, we first show in Lemma D.1 that f pred  

> ∗

is a rational function whose numerator has degree s and whose denominator has degree s + 1. We then use the general Lemma D.2 to prove that s-step self-distillation yields the shrinkage-based estimator bβ f pred 

> ∗

for a suitable choice of regularization and distillation weight parameters. Combining these results, we conclude that s-step self-distillation yields a shrinkage estima-tor that uniquely minimizes the limiting prediction risk over our class of spectral shrinkage es-timators. Furthermore, we prove that it strictly outperforms known estimators, including ridge regression, principal components regression, minimum-norm interpolation, and gradient descent with early stopping. The proofs of this strict dominance over the known estimators are deferred to Appendix F and G, respectively. 24 5.2 Proof Outline of Theorem 3.9

This optimization problem can be thought of as a multivariate gen-eralization of the optimization problem elaborated in Step II of Section 5.1 and consequently is significantly more involved to establish. 

# 6 Discussion 

We establish for spiked covariance matrices that self-distillation achieves optimal prediction error among spectral shrinkage estimators. Notably, for s spikes, s steps of self-distillation are necessary and sufficient, suggesting that each distillation step captures or corrects for a distinct spike direc-tion in the covariance. The main theorems are stated under mild nondegeneracy assumptions (Assumption 2.3), but several boundary regimes, when the assumption is violated, are also worth understanding. For example, when ω0 = 0, then β0 belongs to the span of the spike directions 

{v1, · · · , vs}. In this case, or if two spike strengths (δi, δj) satisfy δiδj = cσ40 , self-distillation still yields the optimal shrinkage, but the number of distillation steps required can be strictly smaller than s and/or we possibly need to start from the 0 estimator instead of a ridge regularized esti-mator. Similarly, if multiple spikes have the same magnitude, then the optimal number of steps 25 would be ℓ, the number of distinct spike strengths. Thus, the relevant complexity is not always the raw number of spikes, but rather an effective number of spectral degrees of freedom. Our argu-ments extend to these settings, but the bookkeeping becomes substantially more involved without introducing new intuition, thus we refrain from presenting these here. Several outstanding questions remain following our paper. A natural direction is to determine whether the optimal shrinkage rule admits a Bayesian interpretation (e.g., whether it is Bayes-optimal under a suitable prior) and can be recovered through an iterated Bayesian updating pro-cedure. While our theory establishes optimality within the spectral shrinkage class, it remains important to clarify what makes the self-distillation parametrization statistically or algorithmi-cally preferable to directly fitting a general rational shrinkage function. Finally, extending our framework beyond the current ℓ2-regularized linear model to more general settings — such as 

ℓ1- or ℓp-regularized models, more general convex penalized linear or generalized linear models, random-feature models, or simple neural networks — would help determine how universal the optimality of the self-distillation phenomenon is. Our simulations for ℓ1-penalized regression al-ready suggest that self-distillation can improve upon the corresponding standard estimator, but developing a complete theoretical understanding in this setting remains an interesting open prob-lem for future work.

A.3 Deterministic Equivalents 

In this section, we recall the notion of deterministic equivalents , where we approximate a random matrix by a deterministic one, which is asymptotically close to the random matrix when tested against a broad class of matrices (see Chapter 2 of [80] for a detailed discussion). This perspective is very common in modern random matrix theory and its applications to statistical problems; see, e.g., [12, 29, 81–83]. 35 Definition A.3 (Deterministic equivalence) . Let An and Bn be (possibly non-symmetric and random) matrix sequences of growing dimension. We write An ≍ Bn if for every deterministic sequence of test matrices Θn with uniformly bounded trace norm ∥Θn∥∗ := tr (√Θ⊤Θ) ,

# F f pred 

∗ versus other Estimators - Proofs of Theorems 3.5(a), (b) and (c) 

As discussed in Section 3, many of the common estimators used in practice correspond to or are well-approximated by simple shrinkage rules. In this section, we prove Theorem 3.5 parts (a), (b) and (c) i.e., the shrinkage rule f pred  

> ∗

we computed in Appendix B achieves a strictly smaller loss than the min-norm interpolator, self-distillation shrinkage rules that use the same λ for all rounds of distillation (in particular Ridge Regression) and finally, early stopped gradient descent.

# I Decentralized Learning - Proof of Theorem 3.9 

I.1 Notation and The Bias–Variance Decomposition 

We begin by rewriting the aggregated estimator in a form that absorbs the aggregation weights into the shrinkage functions themselves, which makes the subsequent bias–variance decomposi-tion more transparent. Throughout, the index ℓ refers to a client and ranges over {1, . . . , K}, the index j refers to a spike and ranges over {1, . . . , s}, and the index i refers to an eigenvector and ranges over {1, . . . , p}. For each client ℓ, the corresponding spectral shrinkage estimator is

.Lemma I.1 is useful because it expresses the aggregated estimator as an average of K terms with the same normalization. This allows the conditional prediction risk to be decomposed into a bias term and K separate variance contributions. The proof is a straightforward algebraic manipulation and follows the first part of Section B.2. We omit the details. Using these notations, we can deduce a standard bias–variance decomposition of the prediction risk of the aggregate estimator.

# J Quadratic Forms of Products - Proof of Theorem 3.10 

In this section, we introduce several technical results needed to prove Theorem 3.10. Recall m(z)

and mδ(z) from Section A.1 and Lemma A.11. Since Theorem 3.10 allows the two matrices to 96 have different aspect ratios cℓ and ck, we use the subscripts mcℓ (z), mck (z) and mδ, cℓ (z), mδ, ck (z) to denote the corresponding Stieltjes transforms throughout this section. We start by computing limits of products of resolvents of independent covariance matrices in the following preliminary Lemma. 

Lemma J.1. Let

We then extend this to simulta-neous convergence over all z using the following theorem, due to Vitali and Porter; for a proof, see Section 2.4 in [87]. 

Theorem J.2. Let D ⊆ C be a connected open set and { fn} be a locally bounded sequence of analytic functions on D. If lim n→∞ fn(z) exists for each z belonging to a set E ⊆ D which has an accumulation point in D, then { fn} converges uniformly on compact subsets of D to an analytic function. 

The key argument to prove Theorem 3.10 is that we can extend Lemma J.1 to general functions in F . The proof of the theorem proceeds in two steps. First, we extend Lemma J.1 from first-order resolvents to arbitrary powers of resolvents. For this first step, we use the following standard Lemma from complex analysis, which is commonly employed in the study of high-dimensional ridge asymptotics; see, for instance, [29, 83, 86]. For a proof, see Lemma 2.14 in [85]. 

Lemma J.3. Let D ⊆ C be a connected open set, and let f n : D → C be analytic for each n. Assume that the sequence ( fn) is uniformly bounded on D, that is, there exists M > 0 such that 

| fn(z)| ≤ M for all n and all z ∈ D.
